<meta name="DC.Title" content="Genomic Analysis of Organismal Complexity in the Multicellular Green Alga Volvox carteri" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1126/science.1188800" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2010-07-09" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Association for the Advancement of Science" />

<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="restricted" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="The volvocine algae include both the unicellular Chlamydomonas and the multicellular Volvox , which diverged from one another 50 to 200 million years ago. Prochnik et al. (p. [223][1]) compared the Volvox genome with that of Chlamydomonas to identify any genomic innovations that might have been associated with the transition to multicellularity. Size changes were observed in several protein families in Volvox , but, overall, the Volvox genome and predicted proteome were highly similar to those of Chlamydomonas . Thus, biological complexity can arise without major changes in genome content or protein domains.

The multicellular green alga Volvox carteri and its morphologically diverse close relatives (the volvocine algae) are well suited for the investigation of the evolution of multicellularity and development. We sequenced the 138–mega–base pair genome of V. carteri and compared its ~14,500 predicted proteins to those of its unicellular relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii . Despite fundamental differences in organismal complexity and life history, the two species have similar protein-coding potentials and few species-specific protein-coding gene predictions. Volvox is enriched in volvocine-algal–specific proteins, including those associated with an expanded and highly compartmentalized extracellular matrix. Our analysis shows that increases in organismal complexity can be associated with modifications of lineage-specific proteins rather than large-scale invention of protein-coding capacity.

 [1]: /lookup/volpage/329/223?iss=5988" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;The multicellular green alga &lt;i&gt;Volvox carteri&lt;/i&gt; and its morphologically diverse close relatives (the volvocine algae) are well suited for the investigation of the evolution of multicellularity and development. We sequenced the 138–mega–base pair genome of &lt;i&gt;V. carteri&lt;/i&gt; and compared its ~14,500 predicted proteins to those of its unicellular relative &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas reinhardtii&lt;/i&gt;. Despite fundamental differences in organismal complexity and life history, the two species have similar protein-coding potentials and few species-specific protein-coding gene predictions. &lt;i&gt;Volvox&lt;/i&gt; is enriched in  volvocine-algal–specific proteins, including those associated with an expanded and highly compartmentalized extracellular matrix. Our analysis shows that increases in organismal complexity can be associated with modifications of lineage-specific proteins rather than large-scale invention of protein-coding capacity.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="editor" content="&lt;h3&gt;Going Multicellular&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The volvocine algae include both the unicellular &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/i&gt; and the multicellular &lt;i&gt;Volvox&lt;/i&gt;, which diverged from one another 50 to 200 million years ago. &lt;b&gt;Prochnik &lt;i&gt;et al.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/b&gt; (p. 223) compared the &lt;i&gt;Volvox&lt;/i&gt; genome with that of &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/i&gt; to identify any genomic innovations that might have been associated with the transition to multicellularity. Size changes were observed in several protein families in &lt;i&gt;Volvox&lt;/i&gt;, but, overall, the &lt;i&gt;Volvox&lt;/i&gt; genome and predicted proteome were highly similar to those of &lt;i&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/i&gt;. Thus, biological complexity can arise without major changes in genome content or protein domains.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Genomic Analysis of Organismal Complexity in the Multicellular Green Alga Volvox carteri" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/329/5988/223/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="The volvocine algae include both the unicellular Chlamydomonas and the multicellular Volvox , which diverged from one another 50 to 200 million years ago. Prochnik et al. (p. [223][1]) compared the Volvox genome with that of Chlamydomonas to identify any genomic innovations that might have been associated with the transition to multicellularity. Size changes were observed in several protein families in Volvox , but, overall, the Volvox genome and predicted proteome were highly similar to those of Chlamydomonas . Thus, biological complexity can arise without major changes in genome content or protein domains.

The multicellular green alga Volvox carteri and its morphologically diverse close relatives (the volvocine algae) are well suited for the investigation of the evolution of multicellularity and development. We sequenced the 138–mega–base pair genome of V. carteri and compared its ~14,500 predicted proteins to those of its unicellular relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii . Despite fundamental differences in organismal complexity and life history, the two species have similar protein-coding potentials and few species-specific protein-coding gene predictions. Volvox is enriched in volvocine-algal–specific proteins, including those associated with an expanded and highly compartmentalized extracellular matrix. Our analysis shows that increases in organismal complexity can be associated with modifications of lineage-specific proteins rather than large-scale invention of protein-coding capacity.

 [1]: /lookup/volpage/329/223?iss=5988" />

Size changes were observed in several protein families in Volvox , but, overall, the Volvox genome and predicted proteome were highly similar to those of Chlamydomonas . Thus, biological complexity can arise without major changes in genome content or protein domains.

The multicellular green alga Volvox carteri and its morphologically diverse close relatives (the volvocine algae) are well suited for the investigation of the evolution of multicellularity and development. We sequenced the 138–mega–base pair genome of V. carteri and compared its ~14,500 predicted proteins to those of its unicellular relative Chlamydomonas reinhardtii .     <script id="loadcss">
      /*! CSS rel=preload polyfill. Depends on loadCSS function. [c]2016 @scottjehl, Filament Group, Inc. Licensed MIT  */
(function( w ){
  // rel=preload support test
  if( !w.loadCSS ){
    return;
  }
  var rp = loadCSS.relpreload = {};
  rp.support = function(){
    try {
      return w.document.createElement( "link" ).relList.supports( "preload" );
    } catch (e) {
      return false;
    }
  };

  // loop preload links and fetch using loadCSS
  rp.poly = function(){
    var links = w.document.getElementsByTagName( "link" );
    for( var i = 0; i < links.length; i++ ){
      var link = links[ i ];
      if( link.rel === "preload" && link.getAttribute( "as" ) === "style" ){
        w.loadCSS( link.href, link );
        link.rel = null;
      }
    }
  };

  // if link[rel=preload] is not supported, we must fetch the CSS manually using loadCSS
  if( !rp.support() ){
    rp.poly();
    var run = w.setInterval( rp.poly, 300 );
    if( w.addEventListener ){
      w.addEventListener( "load", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } );
    }
    if( w.attachEvent ){
      w.attachEvent( "onload", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } )
    }
  }
}( this ));
    </script>

<div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container article__body">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_sci_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_sci_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" data-highwire-glossary-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section editor-summary" id="abstract-1"><h2 class="">Going Multicellular</h2><p id="p-2">The volvocine algae include both the unicellular <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and the multicellular <em>Volvox</em>, which diverged from one another 50 to 200 million years ago. <strong>Prochnik <em>et al.</em></strong> (p. <a href="/lookup/volpage/329/223?iss=5988">223</a>) compared the <em>Volvox</em> genome with that of <em>Chlamydomonas</em> to identify any genomic innovations that might have been associated with the transition to multicellularity. Size changes were observed in several protein families in <em>Volvox</em>, but, overall, the <em>Volvox</em> genome and predicted proteome were highly similar to those of <em>Chlamydomonas</em>. Thus, biological complexity can arise without major changes in genome content or protein domains.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-3"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-4">The multicellular green alga <em>Volvox carteri</em> and its morphologically diverse close relatives (the volvocine algae) are well suited for the investigation of the evolution of multicellularity and development. We sequenced the 138–mega–base pair genome of <em>V. carteri</em> and compared its ~14,500 predicted proteins to those of its unicellular relative <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>. Despite fundamental differences in organismal complexity and life history, the two species have similar protein-coding potentials and few species-specific protein-coding gene predictions. <em>Volvox</em> is enriched in  volvocine-algal–specific proteins, including those associated with an expanded and highly compartmentalized extracellular matrix. Our analysis shows that increases in organismal complexity can be associated with modifications of lineage-specific proteins rather than large-scale invention of protein-coding capacity.</p></div><p id="p-5">Multicellularity and cellular differentiation evolved independently in diverse lineages, including green and red algae, animals, fungi, plants, Amoebozoa, and Chromalveolates (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>) (fig. S1A), yet the genetic changes that underlie these transitions remain poorly understood. The volvocine algae, which include both unicellular and multicellular species with various levels of morphological and developmental complexity, are an appealing model for studying such an evolutionary transition (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>) [fig. S2 and supporting online material (SOM) text]. Multicellular <em>Volvox carteri</em> (hereafter <em>Volvox</em>) has two cell types: ~2000 small biflagellate somatic cells that are embedded in the surface of a transparent sphere of glycoprotein-rich extracellular matrix (ECM), and ~16 large reproductive cells (termed gonidia) that lie just below the somatic cell monolayer (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1A</a> and figs. S1B and S3). The somatic cells resemble those of <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>, a model unicellular volvocine alga (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1B</a>). The changes that are associated with the evolution of <em>Volvox</em> from a <em>Chlamydomonas</em>-like unicellular ancestor have clear parallels in other multicellular lineages but took place more recently than in land plants and animals (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F1">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Volvox and Chlamydomonas. (A) Adult Volvox is composed of ~2000 Chlamydomonas-like somatic cells (s) and ~16 large germline gonidia (g) (scale bar, 200 &#x3BC;m) (fig. S1B). (B) Chlamydomonas cell showing apical flagella (f), chloroplast (c), and eyespot (e) (scale bar, 10 &#x3BC;m). The microscopy used is described in (5)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-240365633" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 1&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-6&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Adult &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; is composed of ~2000 &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt;-like somatic cells (s) and ~16 large germline gonidia (g) (scale bar, 200 &#x3BC;m) (fig. S1B). (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;) &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt; cell showing apical flagella (f), chloroplast (c), and eyespot (e) (scale bar, 10 &#x3BC;m). The microscopy used is described in (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-5-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-5&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;5&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F1.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F1-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F1.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 1"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/663501" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F1-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 1</span> <p id="p-6" class="first-child"><em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em>. (<strong>A</strong>) Adult <em>Volvox</em> is composed of ~2000 <em>Chlamydomonas</em>-like somatic cells (s) and ~16 large germline gonidia (g) (scale bar, 200 μm) (fig. S1B). (<strong>B</strong>) <em>Chlamydomonas</em> cell showing apical flagella (f), chloroplast (c), and eyespot (e) (scale bar, 10 μm). The microscopy used is described in (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-7">To begin to characterize the genomic features that are associated with volvocine multicellularity, we sequenced the 138–mega–base pair (Mbp) <em>Volvox</em> genome to ~11.1× redundant coverage (~2.9 million reads) using a whole-genome shotgun strategy (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). The assembly captures over 98% of known mRNA sequences and expressed sequence tags (ESTs) (<a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). The <em>Volvox</em> nuclear genome is 19.6 Mbp (17%) larger than the <em>Chlamydomonas</em> genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), primarily because of increased repeat content in <em>Volvox</em> relative to <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (<a id="xref-ref-5-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) (table S1). Whereas a few repeat families show bursts of expansion in the <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> lineages, most have changed gradually (fig. S4) (<a id="xref-ref-5-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1</span> <p id="p-8" class="first-child">Comparison of the <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> genomes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><div class="table-inline"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li><a href="/highwire/markup/663507/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li><li><a href="" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/663507/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_embed" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li></ul></div></div></div><p id="p-10">The sequence divergence between <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> is comparable to that between human and chicken [which diverged ~310 million years ago (Ma)], human and frog (~350 Ma), and <em>Arabidopsis</em> and poplar (~110 Ma), based on the frequency of synonymous transversions at fourfold-degenerate sites (4DTV distance) (<a id="xref-ref-5-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6"><em>6</em></a>) (table S2). Although conserved synteny between <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> genomes is evident, these volvocine algae show higher rates of genomic rearrangement than vertebrates and eudicots do (tables S2 to S4 and fig. S5).</p><p id="p-11">We predicted 14,566 proteins (at 14,520 loci) in <em>Volvox</em> (<a id="xref-ref-5-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and tables S5 to S7). <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> have similar numbers of genes (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>) and more genes than most unicellular organisms (table S8). Genes in both algae are intron-rich (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-4" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), like those of most multicellular organisms (table S8), and introns are longer, on average, in <em>Volvox</em> (fig. S6) (<a id="xref-ref-5-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). Novel protein domains and/or combinations are proposed to have contributed to multicellularity in metazoans (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>), and such expansions are evident in both the plant and animal lineages (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a> and table S9). In contrast, the numbers of domains and combinations in <em>Volvox</em> are very similar to those in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and other unicellular species (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a> and table S9) (<a id="xref-ref-5-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). microRNAs (miRNAs) have been identified in <em>Chlamydomonas</em>, most of which have no homologs in <em>Volvox</em> (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>). It is likely that <em>Volvox</em> also has miRNAs, but these have yet to be characterized.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F2">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparisons of protein domains and families. (A) Total number of Pfam domains in the multicellular (green) and unicellular (blue) species: Ostreococcus tauri (ota); Micromonas pusilla (mpu); Chlamydomonas reinhardtii (cre); Volvox carteri (vca); Physcomitrella patens (ppa); Arabidopsis thaliana (ath); Thalassiosira pseudonana (tps); Phaeodactylum tricornutum (ptr); Monosiga brevicollis (mbr); Nematostella vectensis (nve); and Homo sapiens (hsa). (B) The numbers of protein families from Volvox, Chlamydomonas, and other species (5) are shown in a Venn diagram. The numbers of Volvox and Chlamydomonas members per protein family are plotted for all families (C) and for the volvocine algae&#x2013;specific subset (D). In these density plots, the position of each square represents the number of family members in Volvox (x axis) and Chlamydomonas (y axis), with coloring to indicate the total number of families plotted at each position. The Pfam domains for outlier families are abbreviated as follows: a, ankyrin repeat; c, cysteine protease; g, gametolysin; h, histone; L, LRR." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-240365633" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 2&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-12&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Comparisons of protein domains and families. (&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Total number of Pfam domains in the multicellular (green) and unicellular (blue) species: &lt;em&gt;Ostreococcus tauri&lt;/em&gt; (ota); &lt;em&gt;Micromonas pusilla&lt;/em&gt; (mpu); &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas reinhardtii&lt;/em&gt; (cre); &lt;em&gt;Volvox carteri&lt;/em&gt; (vca); &lt;em&gt;Physcomitrella patens&lt;/em&gt; (ppa); &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/em&gt; (ath); &lt;em&gt;Thalassiosira pseudonana&lt;/em&gt; (tps); &lt;em&gt;Phaeodactylum tricornutum&lt;/em&gt; (ptr); &lt;em&gt;Monosiga brevicollis&lt;/em&gt; (mbr); &lt;em&gt;Nematostella vectensis&lt;/em&gt; (nve); and &lt;em&gt;Homo sapiens&lt;/em&gt; (hsa). (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;) The numbers of protein families from &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt;, and other species (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-5-10&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-5&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;5&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) are shown in a Venn diagram. The numbers of &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt; members per protein family are plotted for all families (&lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) and for the volvocine algae&#x2013;specific subset (&lt;strong&gt;D&lt;/strong&gt;). In these density plots, the position of each square represents the number of family members in &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;x&lt;/em&gt; axis) and &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;y&lt;/em&gt; axis), with coloring to indicate the total number of families plotted at each position. The Pfam domains for outlier families are abbreviated as follows: a, ankyrin repeat; c, cysteine protease; g, gametolysin; h, histone; L, LRR.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F2.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F2.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 2"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/663502" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F2-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 2</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Comparisons of protein domains and families. (<strong>A</strong>) Total number of Pfam domains in the multicellular (green) and unicellular (blue) species: <em>Ostreococcus tauri</em> (ota); <em>Micromonas pusilla</em> (mpu); <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em> (cre); <em>Volvox carteri</em> (vca); <em>Physcomitrella patens</em> (ppa); <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (ath); <em>Thalassiosira pseudonana</em> (tps); <em>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</em> (ptr); <em>Monosiga brevicollis</em> (mbr); <em>Nematostella vectensis</em> (nve); and <em>Homo sapiens</em> (hsa). (<strong>B</strong>) The numbers of protein families from <em>Volvox</em>, <em>Chlamydomonas</em>, and other species (<a id="xref-ref-5-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) are shown in a Venn diagram. The numbers of <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> members per protein family are plotted for all families (<strong>C</strong>) and for the volvocine algae–specific subset (<strong>D</strong>). In these density plots, the position of each square represents the number of family members in <em>Volvox</em> (<em>x</em> axis) and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (<em>y</em> axis), with coloring to indicate the total number of families plotted at each position. The Pfam domains for outlier families are abbreviated as follows: a, ankyrin repeat; c, cysteine protease; g, gametolysin; h, histone; L, LRR.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-13">To investigate protein evolution in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and <em>Volvox</em>, we constructed families containing both orthologs and paralogs from 20 diverse species, including animals, plants, fungi, protists, and bacteria (<a id="xref-ref-5-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) (table S10). We assigned 9311 (64%) <em>Volvox</em> and 9189 (63%) <em>Chlamydomonas</em> protein sequences to 7780 families (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2B</a>), of which 80% (5423) contain one ortholog from each alga (table S11). 1835 families (26%) contain orthologs only from <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (that is, they are volvocine-specific) (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2B</a>). Only 32 EST-supported <em>Volvox</em> gene models lack detectable homologs in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> or other species (<a id="xref-ref-5-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) (tables S12 and S13), suggesting that limited protein-coding innovation occurred in the <em>Volvox</em> lineage.</p><p id="p-14">Gene-family expansion or contraction is an important source of adaptive variation (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10"><em>10</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>). In a density plot of proteins per family in <em>Volvox</em> versus <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>), most points lie on or near the diagonal, showing that the majority of families have approximately equal membership from each alga. Exceptions include the gametolysin/VMP (<em>Volvox</em> matrix metalloprotease) family, whose substrates are cell-wall/ECM proteins (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>, g), and a family containing leucine-rich repeat proteins (LRRs), whose functions in green algae have not been well defined (<a id="xref-fig-2-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>, L). Conversely, families containing core histones and ankyrin repeats have more members in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (<a id="xref-fig-2-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2C</a>, a). In contrast, the subset of 1835 volvocine algae–specific families (<a id="xref-ref-5-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) shows a strikingly different distribution (<a id="xref-fig-2-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2D</a>), with a significant bias toward more members in <em>Volvox</em> (<em>P</em> = 2 × 10<sup>–120</sup>, heterogeneity chi-squared test). These families include ECM proteins such as VMPs and pherophorins that both participate in ECM biogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>), and an algal subgroup of cysteine proteases (<a id="xref-fig-2-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2D</a>, c).</p><p id="p-15">Although some of the genomic differences between <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> may reflect environmental adaptations that have not been extensively investigated (<a id="xref-ref-5-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>), we expected many of the changes to be in protein families that are associated with the large differences in organismal complexity. Therefore, we investigated in detail pathways related to key developmental processes that are either novel or qualitatively different in <em>Volvox</em> relative to <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (<a id="xref-ref-2-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>). These include the following: protein secretion and membrane trafficking [potentially involved in cytoplasmic bridge formation via incomplete cytokinesis (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14"><em>14</em></a>)]; the cytoskeleton [potentially involved in <em>Volvox</em>-specific basal body rotation, inversion, and asymmetric cell division (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>)]; ECM and cell-wall proteins [involved in ECM expansion, sexual differentiation, and morphogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-12-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>) (fig. S2)]; and cell-cycle regulation (potentially involved in cell division patterning or asymmetric cell division). The components of these pathways are nearly identical in <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (table S14). Transcription-related proteins also have highly similar repertoires in the two species (fig. S7 and table S15) (<a id="xref-ref-5-15" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>). Thus, with three exceptions (see below), we found little difference in the complements of proteins that might underlie developmental complexity in <em>Volvox</em>.</p><p id="p-16">The ECM composes up to 99% of an adult <em>Volvox</em> spheroid and is larger and more structurally complex than the ancestral <em>Chlamydomonas</em>-like cell wall from which it was derived (<a id="xref-ref-12-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>) (fig. S3 and SOM text). These changes are mirrored by at least two dramatic changes in ECM protein family size in <em>Volvox</em> as compared with <em>Chlamydomonas</em>: pherophorins (49 versus 27 members) and VMPs (42 versus 8 members) (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3A</a>, fig. S8, and table S14). We found expanded <em>Volvox</em>-specific clades of pherophorins and VMPs as well as species-specific duplications in both algae (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3A</a> and fig. S8). Besides their role in ECM structure, <em>Volvox</em> pherophorins have evolved into a diffusible sex-inducer glycoprotein that has replaced nitrogen deprivation (used in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and other volvocine algae) as the trigger for sexual differentiation (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>). The co-option of an ECM protein for sexual signaling shows parallels in the sexual agglutinins of <em>Chlamydomonas</em> that are themselves related to cell-wall/ECM proteins (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). The <em>Volvox</em> ECM proteins, pherophorins, and VMPs diversified and then presumably were recruited to novel developmental roles in <em>Volvox</em>, thus representing a source of adaptive plasticity that is specific to the volvocine algae.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F3">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Diversification of key protein families with known or predicted roles in Volvox development. Unrooted maximum likelihood trees (5) are shown for pherophorins (A) and cyclins (B). Protein sequences are from Volvox (Vc, green) and Chlamydomonas (Cr, blue). Incomplete gene models were not included; Volvox-specific clades with poorly resolved branches are collapsed into triangles; bootstrap support &#x2265;50% is indicated on branches. Red asterisks indicate pherophorins whose mRNA levels are up-regulated by a sex inducer (16)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-240365633" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 3&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-17&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;Diversification of key protein families with known or predicted roles in &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; development. Unrooted maximum likelihood trees (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-5-16&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-5&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;5&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) are shown for pherophorins (&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) and cyclins (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;). Protein sequences are from &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt; (Vc, green) and &lt;em&gt;Chlamydomonas&lt;/em&gt; (Cr, blue). Incomplete gene models were not included; &lt;em&gt;Volvox&lt;/em&gt;-specific clades with poorly resolved branches are collapsed into triangles; bootstrap support &#x2265;50% is indicated on branches. Red asterisks indicate pherophorins whose mRNA levels are up-regulated by a sex inducer (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-16-2&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-16&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;16&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F3.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F3.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 3"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/329/5988/223/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/663505" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F3-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 3</span> <p id="p-17" class="first-child">Diversification of key protein families with known or predicted roles in <em>Volvox</em> development. Unrooted maximum likelihood trees (<a id="xref-ref-5-16" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>) are shown for pherophorins (<strong>A</strong>) and cyclins (<strong>B</strong>). Protein sequences are from <em>Volvox</em> (Vc, green) and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> (Cr, blue). Incomplete gene models were not included; <em>Volvox</em>-specific clades with poorly resolved branches are collapsed into triangles; bootstrap support ≥50% is indicated on branches. Red asterisks indicate pherophorins whose mRNA levels are up-regulated by a sex inducer (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-18">The <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> cell cycles are fundamentally similar, but <em>Volvox</em> has evolved additional regulation of timing, number, and types of cell divisions (symmetric and asymmetric) among different subsets of embryonic cells (<a id="xref-ref-2-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>). The division program of males and females is further modified during sexual development to produce sperm and eggs (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>). Whereas most of the core cell-cycle proteins of <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> have a 1:1 orthology relationship, the cyclin D family is notably larger in <em>Volvox</em>. In addition to three D cyclins that have <em>Chlamydomonas</em> orthologs (Cycd2, Cycd3, and Cycd4), <em>Volvox</em> has four D1-related cyclins (Cycd1.1 to Cycd1.4), whereas <em>Chlamydomonas</em> has only one (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3B</a>). D cyclins bind cyclin-dependent kinases and target them to phosphorylate retinoblastoma (RB)–related proteins (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19"><em>19</em></a>). In <em>Chlamydomonas</em>, the RB-related protein MAT3 controls the timing and extent of cell division (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>), so it is plausible that the expanded D-type cyclin family in <em>Volvox</em> plays a role in regulating its cell division program during development<span class="strike">.</span></p><p id="p-19">The genetic changes that brought about the evolution of multicellular life from unicellular progenitors remain obscure (<a id="xref-ref-2-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22"><em>22</em></a>). For example, many proteins associated with animal multicellularity, such as cadherins and receptor tyrosine kinases (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23"><em>23</em></a>), evolved in the unicellular ancestor of animals and are specific to its descendants. Other critical components of metazoan multicellularity, including key transcription factors and signaling molecules, are absent from the closest unicellular relatives of animals (<a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22"><em>22</em></a>), suggesting that animal multicellularity also involved protein-coding innovation. Our comparisons of <em>Volvox</em> and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> indicate that, with the interesting exceptions of pherophorins, VMPs, and D cyclins, the developmental innovations in the <em>Volvox</em> lineage did not involve major changes in the ancestral protein repertoire. This is consistent with previous observations indicating co-option of ancestral genes into new developmental processes without changes in copy number or function (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24"><em>24</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>). However, our analyses do suggest that the expansion of lineage-specific proteins occurred preferentially in <em>Volvox</em> and provided a key source of developmental innovation and adaptation. Further studies of gene regulation (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27"><em>27</em></a>) and the role of noncoding RNAs (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28"><em>28</em></a>) will be enabled by the <em>Volvox</em> genome sequence, allowing a more complete understanding of the transformation from a cellularly complex <em>Chlamydomonas</em>-like ancestor to a morphologically and developmentally complex “fierce roller.”</p><div class="section app" id="app-1"><h2 class="">Supporting Online Material</h2><div id="DC1" class="supplementary-material"><p id="p-20"><a href="http://www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/329/5988/223/DC1">www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/329/5988/223/DC1</a></p><p id="p-21">Materials and Methods</p><p id="p-22">SOM Text</p><p id="p-23">Figs. S1 to S8</p><p id="p-24">Tables S1 to S16</p><p id="p-25">References</p></div></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><ul><li class="fn" id="fn-1"><p id="p-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-1-1">↵</a><span class="fn-label">*</span> These authors contributed equally to this work.</p></li></ul></div><div class="section ref-list" id="ref-list-1"><h2 class="">References and Notes</h2><ol class="cit-list"><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-1-1" title="View reference 1 in text" id="ref-1">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-journal" id="cit-329.5988.223.1" data-doi="10.1126/science.1085544"><div class="cit-metadata"><ol class="cit-auth-list"><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">S.     <div class="media__body">
        <h3 class="highwire-cite-title-wrapper media__headline"><a href="/content/329/5988/223/suppl/DC1" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="highwire-cite-title media__headline__title">Genomic Analysis of Organismal Complexity in the Multicellular Green Alga <em>Volvox carteri</em></div></a></h3>
            <p  class="highwire-cite-authors byline">By <span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Simon E.             <p  class="highwire-cite-metadata byline"><em>Science</em><time>09 Jul 2010</time> : 223-226 </p>
                <div class="highwire-cite-snippet media__deck">
      <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="section precis" id="abstract-2" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><p id="p-3">Comparison of the <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and <em>Volvox</em> genomes show few differences, despite their divergent life histories.</p></div></div>    </div>

</ul>  </div>
</article></div>  </div>

      <ul class="media-grid media-grid--2 media-grid--minor"><li class="first"><div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-pisa="sci;371/6526/225" data-pisa-master="sci;371/6526/225" data-apath="/sci/371/6526/225.atom" id="sci3716526225atom"><h3 class="overline">Feature</h3>

  <a href="/content/371/6526/239" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="highwire-cite-title">Tracking, targeting, and conserving soil biodiversity</span></a></div></li>
<li><div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-pisa="sci;371/6526/242" data-pisa-master="sci;371/6526/242" data-apath="/sci/371/6526/242.atom" id="sci3716526242atom"><h3 class="overline">Climate Science</h3>
  <a href="/content/371/6526/242" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="highwire-cite-title">Fighting the good fight</span></a></div></li>
<li><div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-pisa="sci;371/6526/216" data-pisa-master="sci;371/6526/216" data-apath="/sci/371/6526/216.atom" id="sci3716526216atom"><h3 class="overline">SCI COMMUN</h3>

<li><div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-pisa="sci;371/6526/230" data-pisa-master="sci;371/6526/230" data-apath="/sci/371/6526/230.atom" id="sci3716526230atom"><h3 class="overline">Coronavirus</h3>
